here:\n\n```text\n/media/hidayat/PersonalData/Kali_Linux_Files/AndroidStudioPanda2Additional/.android/avd/Pixel_5_API_31.avd\n```\n\n### Verification\n\nThe emulator list now includes:\n\n```text\nPixel_5_API_31\nPixel_6\nPixel_7\n```\n\nThe emulator booted successfully and Android reported:\n\n```text\nboot_completed=1\n```\n\nThe active Android launcher was:\n\n```text\ncom.google.android.apps.nexuslauncher/.NexusLauncherActivity\n```\n\nADB input commands were sent successfully, confirming the virtual phone can be interacted with.\n\n### Beginner Explanation\n\nAn Android Virtual Device, or AVD, is a fake Android phone stored on your computer.\nIt is like a saved VirtualBox machine, but instead of running Windows or Linux, it runs Android.\n\nThe device profile controls the phone shape, screen size, RAM, cameras, and hardware settings.\nThe system image controls the Android version installed inside that fake phone.\nFor this project, `Pixel_5_API_31` means a Pixel 5-style virtual phone running Android 12.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task `P1.S1.SS1.MT3` is complete.\nThe next micro-task is `P1.S1.SS2.MT1 — Create New Android Project`.\n\n### Mem0 Session Memory Repair\n\nBefore starting `P1.S1.SS2.MT1`, the local project memory helper was repaired and verified.\nThe old helper was using a Gemini cloud configuration and a hardcoded API key, which conflicts with the project's local-only AI rule.\n\nThe helper at `knowledge-graph/mem0_helper.py` now:\n\n- uses local Ollama models instead of a cloud provider\n- disables Mem0 telemetry so no PostHog analytics are sent\n- saves session checkpoints with `infer=False` so memory entries are stored directly and quickly\n- stores memory files in stable local paths under `knowledge-graph/`\n\nThree local memories were saved successfully:\n\n- the current project state and current micro-task\n- the beginner-teaching preference\n- the required Android Studio binary path\n\nVerification was done by running a Mem0 search for the current phase and micro-task, and the saved session state was returned correctly.\n\n---\n\n## Micro-task P1.S1.SS2.MT1 — Create New Android Project\n\n### What Was Created\n\nA complete Android project was initialized in the `android-app/` directory. This includes:\n\n- **Gradle Build System:** `settings.gradle.kts`, `build.gradle.kts`, and `libs.versions.toml` (Version Catalog).\n- **App Manifest:** `AndroidManifest.xml` which identifies the app as `com.rushdululilm.app`.\n- **Main Activity:** `MainActivity.kt` set up with Jetpack Compose.\n- **UI Theme:** Custom colors (`Color.kt`), typography (`Type.kt`), and a Material3 theme (`Theme.kt`).\n- **Build Verification:** Successfully ran `./gradlew help` to confirm the project structure is valid.\n\n### Beginner Explanation\n\nCreating a new project is like setting up a new notebook for a class. We've labeled the cover (Package Name), set up the table of contents (Gradle), and wrote the first \"Hello World\" on the first page (MainActivity).\n\n- **Gradle** is our project's manager. It downloads the code we need from the internet and compiles our code into an app.\n- **Jetpack Compose** is how we draw the screen. Instead of using separate design files, we write Kotlin functions that \"compose\" the UI.\n- **The Theme** is our app's style guide. It ensures that every screen uses the same colors and font sizes, making the app look professional and consistent.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task `P1.S1.SS2.MT1` is complete.\nThe next micro-task is `P1.S1.SS2.MT2 — Add Dependencies to build.gradle.kts`.\n\n---\n\n## Micro-task P1.S1.SS2.MT4 — Run the App for the First Time\n\n### What Was Verified\n\nThe Rushd-ul-Ilm app was successfully built, installed, and launched on the `Pixel_5_API_31` emulator.\n\n- **Build Success:** All dependencies and themes are correctly configured.\n- **Launch Success:** The app starts and displays the main greeting screen.\n- **Stability:** Logcat shows no crashes or errors during startup.\n- **Active Screen:** Verified that `MainActivity` is the currently resumed activity on the device.\n\n### Beginner Explanation\n\nRunning the app for the first time is like \"Turning the Key\" in a car you just built. It proves that all the parts we've added so far—the libraries, the code, and the settings—are working together perfectly. \n\nWe used **ADB (Android Debug Bridge)** to push the app from our computer to the virtual phone. Once it was there, we told the phone to \"Open the front door\" (MainActivity). Seeing the \"Hello Android!\" message means our foundation is solid and we are ready to start building the actual features.\n\n### Result\n\nSprint 1.1 is **COMPLETE**.\nThe foundation is laid. We have a working project, a working emulator, and a verified build process.\nThe next phase is **Sprint 1.2 — App Structure & Navigation**.\n\n---\n\n## Micro-task P1.S2.SS1.MT1 — Create Package Folder Structure\n\n### What Was Created\n\nThe standard MVVM architecture folder structure was created inside `com.rushdululilm.app`:\n\n- `ui/screens` — Jetpack Compose screens\n- `ui/components` — Reusable UI components\n- `viewmodel` — MVVM ViewModels\n- `data/local` — Room DB entities and DAOs\n- `data/remote` — Retrofit interfaces\n- `data/repository` — Data repositories\n- `di` — Hilt Dependency Injection modules\n- `utils` — Helper functions\n- `model` — Kotlin data classes\n\nA placeholder Kotlin file with explanatory comments was added to each package so they appear in Android Studio.\n\n### Beginner Explanation\n\nJust like organizing a kitchen with separate drawers for knives, plates, and spices, we organize our code into \"packages\" (folders). This makes it easy to find things as the app grows. MVVM (Model-View-ViewModel) is the standard blueprint for Android apps: \n- `data` (Model) gets the ingredients.\n- `ui` (View) presents the final dish.\n- `viewmodel` acts as the chef, coordinating between them.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task `P1.S2.SS1.MT1` is complete.\nThe next micro-task is `P1.S2.SS1.MT2 — Create Route Constants File`.\n\n---\n\n## Micro-task P1.S2.SS1.MT2 — Create Route Constants File\n\n### What Was Created\n\nA central file for navigation route strings was created:\n- `android-app/app/src/main/java/com/rushdululilm/app/ui/screens/Routes.kt`\n\nThis file contains constants for all planned screens: `HOME`, `ANSWER`, `VIDEO_LIBRARY`, and `SETTINGS`.\n\n### Beginner Explanation\n\nThink of Jetpack Compose Navigation like a city map. Every screen is a different house, and every house needs an address. Instead of typing \"home\" or \"settings\" manually every time we want to change screens, we store them in one file as constants (values that never change). This prevents spelling mistakes and makes the code cleaner.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task `P1.S2.SS1.MT2` is complete.\nThe next micro-task is `P1.S2.SS2.MT1 — Create 4 Empty Placeholder Screens`.\n\n---\n\n## Micro-task P1.S2.SS2.MT1 — Create Placeholder Screens\n\n### What Was Created\n\nFour basic Jetpack Compose screen files were created inside `com.rushdululilm.app.ui.screens`:\n- `HomeScreen.kt`\n- `AnswerScreen.kt`\n- `VideoLibraryScreen.kt`\n- `SettingsScreen.kt`\n\nEach file contains a single `@Composable` function that displays its name as a text placeholder in the center of the screen.\n\n### Beginner Explanation\n\nIn Android, `@Composable` is a special tag that tells the system, \"This function draws a part of the screen.\" Think of it like a painter. When the app wants to show the Home Screen, it calls the `HomeScreen()` function, and that function paints a Box (a container) with some Text inside it. We have created four empty \"canvases\" ready to be filled with our actual app designs.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task `P1.S2.SS2.MT1` is complete.\nThe next micro-task is `P1.S2.SS2.MT2 — Create the Navigation Graph`.\n\n---\n\n## Micro-task P1.S2.SS2.MT2 — Create the Navigation Graph\n\n### What Was Created\n\nA central navigation controller was implemented in `NavGraph.kt`. This file acts as the app's traffic controller, managing the movement between different screens.\n\n- **Navigation Controller:** Created using `rememberNavController()` to track the user's history and change screens.\n- **NavHost:** A frame that swaps between `HomeScreen`, `AnswerScreen`, `VideoLibraryScreen`, and `SettingsScreen` based on the active route.\n- **Bottom Navigation Bar:** A Material3 `NavigationBar` was added to the bottom of the screen with three icons:\n  - **Home:** Navigates to the main mic screen.\n  - **Videos:** Navigates to the searchable video library.\n  - **Settings:** Navigates to app preferences and offline downloads.\n\n### Beginner Explanation\n\nImagine the app is like a house with four rooms.\n- **NavGraph** is the hallway connecting all the rooms.\n- **NavController** is the remote control that lets you choose which room to walk into.\n- **NavHost** is the \"magic door\" that changes its destination when you press a button on the remote.\n\nWe also added a **Bottom Bar** which is like a shortcut panel at the bottom of your phone. It allows you to jump between the Home, Video, and Settings \"rooms\" instantly from anywhere in the app.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task `P1.S2.SS2.MT2` and `MT3` are complete.\nThe app now supports full navigation between its core functional areas.\nThe next sprint is **Sprint 1.3 — Home Screen**.\n\n\n---\n\n## Micro-task P1.S3.SS1.MT1 — Create App Theme (Colors & Typography)\n\n### What Was Created\n\nA custom Material3 theme was established to maintain a cohesive visual identity across the app. This was broken down into three files:\n\n- **`ui/theme/Color.kt`:** Defines the explicit color palette (e.g., IslamicGreen, QuranicBlue, BackgroundCream).\n- **`ui/theme/Type.kt`:** Dictates typography rules. Notably, this enforces a strict accessibility baseline of 16sp for all text.\n- **`ui/theme/Theme.kt`:** Assembles the colors and fonts into a MaterialTheme wrapper (\\`RushdulIlmTheme\\`) that is applied at the root of the UI hierarchy.\n\n### Beginner Explanation\n\nJust as a house needs a defined color palette before painting, an app needs a theme before placing buttons or text. By declaring colors and fonts in one central place, we guarantee that the app looks consistent. If we decide to change the shade of \"Islamic Green,\" we only have to change it in `Color.kt`, and the whole app updates immediately.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task `P1.S3.SS1.MT1` is complete.\nThe app theme is configured.\nThe next micro-task is `P1.S3.SS1.MT2 — Create MicButton Composable Component`.\n\n\n---\n\n## Micro-task P1.S3.SS1.MT2 — Create MicButton Composable Component\n\n### What Was Created\n\nA reusable, interactive microphone button (\\`MicButton.kt\\`) was implemented to serve as the primary action element on the Home screen.\n\n- **Component Design:** The button occupies approximately 40% of the screen height, adhering to the large touch-target requirements.\n- **Dynamic Styling:** It utilizes the established \\`RushdulIlmTheme\\` colors, appearing \\`IslamicGreen\\` by default and switching to \\`ErrorRed\\` when the \\`isRecording\\` state is active.\n- **Animation:** An infinite pulsing animation (\\`rememberInfiniteTransition\\`) is applied when recording to provide clear visual feedback to the user.\n- **Accessibility:** It features bilingual labels (Telugu and English) positioned directly below the button, enforcing the minimum 16sp typography rule.\n- **Dependencies:** Added \\`material-icons-extended\\` to access the required microphone icon.\n\n### Beginner Explanation\n\nThe Mic button is the heart of the app. We built it as a \"Reusable Component\" rather than just drawing it directly on the Home screen. This is like building a pre-fabricated window instead of building a window frame directly into a wall; we can take this pre-built button and easily drop it into any screen we want in the future. The button also has a \"state\" (recording or not recording). When the state changes, the button automatically repaints itself in a different color and starts a pulsing animation, without us needing to manually redraw it.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task \\`P1.S3.SS1.MT2\\` is complete.\nThe MicButton component is ready.\nThe next micro-task is \\`P1.S3.SS1.MT3 — Create LanguageSelector Composable\\`.\n\n\n---\n\n## Micro-task P1.S3.SS1.MT3 — Create LanguageSelector Composable\n\n### What Was Created\n\nA dropdown component (\\`LanguageSelector.kt\\`) was implemented to handle language preferences.\n\n- **Component Design:** Utilizes the recommended Material3 \\`ExposedDropdownMenuBox\\` pattern to provide a clean, modern dropdown experience.\n- **Languages:** Supports Telugu, Urdu, Hindi, and English out of the box.\n- **Accessibility:** Enforces a minimum 56dp touch target for the collapsed selector and 48dp for individual menu items. All text utilizes the 18sp minimum typography defined in the theme.\n- **State Handling:** Takes a \\`selectedLanguage\\` state and an \\`onLanguageSelected\\` lambda to communicate user choices back to the parent ViewModel.\n\n### Beginner Explanation\n\nA \"Dropdown Menu\" in Android used to be complicated to build. Jetpack Compose gives us \\`ExposedDropdownMenuBox\\`, which handles opening, closing, and animating the menu for us. We pass it the currently selected language (so it knows what to show in the box) and a function called \\`onLanguageSelected\\`. When the user taps a different language in the list, the component calls this function to tell the \"brain\" (ViewModel) to update the app.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task \\`P1.S3.SS1.MT3\\` is complete.\nThe LanguageSelector component is ready.\nThe next micro-task is \\`P1.S3.SS1.MT4 — Create SourceSelector Composable\\`.\n\n\n---\n\n## Micro-task P1.S3.SS1.MT4 — Create SourceSelector Composable\n\n### What Was Created\n\nA \\`SourceSelector.kt\\` component was built to allow the user to select their desired Islamic knowledge database.\n\n- **Component Design:** It uses a \\`LazyRow\\` to provide a horizontally scrolling list of options. The items are rendered as \\`FilterChip\\` composables.\n- **Sources Supported:** The component maps the four predefined sources (\"all\", \"islamqa_info\", \"islamqa_org\", \"deoband\") to their readable display names.\n- **Styling:** When a chip is selected, its background changes to \\`IslamicGreen\\` with white text, providing clear visual feedback. Unselected chips retain the default outlined appearance.\n- **Typography:** The section title uses \\`bodyLarge\\` and the chip labels use \\`labelSmall\\` (enforcing the 16sp accessibility minimum).\n\n### Beginner Explanation\n\nWhen asking a question, users might only want fatwas from a specific school of thought or website. The \"Source Selector\" is a horizontal list of buttons (called \"Chips\" in Android). We use a \\`LazyRow\\` which means the list can be scrolled side-to-side, and Android only draws the chips that are currently visible on the screen, saving memory. When a user taps a chip, it turns green, and it tells the \"brain\" (ViewModel) which source to use for the search.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task \\`P1.S3.SS1.MT4\\` is complete.\nThe SourceSelector component is ready.\nThe next micro-task is \\`P1.S3.SS1.MT5 — Create HomeViewModel\\`.\n\n\n---\n\n## Micro-task P1.S3.SS1.MT5 — Create HomeViewModel\n\n### What Was Created\n\nThe `HomeViewModel.kt` was created to serve as the MVVM \"brain\" for the Home screen, managing the UI state and coordinating user actions.\n\n- **Architecture:** It utilizes Hilt (`@HiltViewModel`) for dependency injection, allowing the system to provide it automatically to the `HomeScreen`.\n- **State Management:** Uses Coroutines `StateFlow` to manage:\n  - `selectedLanguage`: Defaults to \"Telugu\".\n  - `selectedSource`: Defaults to \"all\".\n  - `isRecording`: Toggles between true/false.\n  - `uiState`: Tracks the screen phase using a sealed class (`HomeUiState`) with states like `Idle`, `Recording`, `Processing`, `NavigatingToAnswer`, and `Error`.\n- **Action Handlers:** Includes functions (`onMicPressed`, `onLanguageSelected`, `onSourceSelected`) that update these state flows. These act as placeholders for the real offline logic (like AudioRecord API access) that will be integrated in Phase 4.\n\n### Beginner Explanation\n\nThe ViewModel is like the \"waiter\" in a restaurant. The UI (the buttons and menus we just built) is the dining room. When a customer (user) taps a button, the UI tells the waiter \"I want to record.\" The waiter (ViewModel) writes this down on his notepad (`StateFlow`). Because the UI is always watching the waiter`s notepad, it instantly redraws the button in red as soon as the waiter writes \"Recording = true\". This separation means the button doesn`t have to know *how* to record audio; it only knows how to look when recording is happening.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task `P1.S3.SS1.MT5` is complete.\nThe HomeViewModel is ready.\nThe next micro-task is `P1.S3.SS1.MT6 — Build the Full HomeScreen Layout`.\n"
  }
}
```

This is like building a pre-fabricated window instead of building a window frame directly into a wall; we can take this pre-built button and easily drop it into any screen we want in the future. The button also has a \"state\" (recording or not recording). When the state changes, the button automatically repaints itself in a different color and starts a pulsing animation, without us needing to manually redraw it.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task \\`P1.S3.SS1.MT2\\` is complete.\nThe MicButton component is ready.\nThe next micro-task is \\`P1.S3.SS1.MT3 — Create LanguageSelector Composable\\`.\n\n\n---\n\n## Micro-task P1.S3.SS1.MT3 — Create LanguageSelector Composable\n\n### What Was Created\n\nA dropdown component (\\`LanguageSelector.kt\\`) was implemented to handle language preferences.\n\n- **Component Design:** Utilizes the recommended Material3 \\`ExposedDropdownMenuBox\\` pattern to provide a clean, modern dropdown experience.\n- **Languages:** Supports Telugu, Urdu, Hindi, and English out of the box.\n- **Accessibility:** Enforces a minimum 56dp touch target for the collapsed selector and 48dp for individual menu items. All text utilizes the 18sp minimum typography defined in the theme.\n- **State Handling:** Takes a \\`selectedLanguage\\` state and an \\`onLanguageSelected\\` lambda to communicate user choices back to the parent ViewModel.\n\n### Beginner Explanation\n\nA \"Dropdown Menu\" in Android used to be complicated to build. Jetpack Compose gives us \\`ExposedDropdownMenuBox\\`, which handles opening, closing, and animating the menu for us. We pass it the currently selected language (so it knows what to show in the box) and a function called \\`onLanguageSelected\\`. When the user taps a different language in the list, the component calls this function to tell the \"brain\" (ViewModel) to update the app.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task \\`P1.S3.SS1.MT3\\` is complete.\nThe LanguageSelector component is ready.\nThe next micro-task is \\`P1.S3.SS1.MT4 — Create SourceSelector Composable\\`.\n\n\n---\n\n## Micro-task P1.S3.SS1.MT4 — Create SourceSelector Composable\n\n### What Was Created\n\nA \\`SourceSelector.kt\\` component was built to allow the user to select their desired Islamic knowledge database.\n\n- **Component Design:** It uses a \\`LazyRow\\` to provide a horizontally scrolling list of options. The items are rendered as \\`FilterChip\\` composables.\n- **Sources Supported:** The component maps the four predefined sources (\"all\", \"islamqa_info\", \"islamqa_org\", \"deoband\") to their readable display names.\n- **Styling:** When a chip is selected, its background changes to \\`IslamicGreen\\` with white text, providing clear visual feedback. Unselected chips retain the default outlined appearance.\n- **Typography:** The section title uses \\`bodyLarge\\` and the chip labels use \\`labelSmall\\` (enforcing the 16sp accessibility minimum).\n\n### Beginner Explanation\n\nWhen asking a question, users might only want fatwas from a specific school of thought or website. The \"Source Selector\" is a horizontal list of buttons (called \"Chips\" in Android). We use a \\`LazyRow\\` which means the list can be scrolled side-to-side, and Android only draws the chips that are currently visible on the screen, saving memory. When a user taps a chip, it turns green, and it tells the \"brain\" (ViewModel) which source to use for the search.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task \\`P1.S3.SS1.MT4\\` is complete.\nThe SourceSelector component is ready.\nThe next micro-task is \\`P1.S3.SS1.MT5 — Create HomeViewModel\\`.\n\n\n---\n\n## Micro-task P1.S3.SS1.MT5 — Create HomeViewModel\n\n### What Was Created\n\nThe `HomeViewModel.kt` was created to serve as the MVVM \"brain\" for the Home screen, managing the UI state and coordinating user actions.\n\n- **Architecture:** It utilizes Hilt (`@HiltViewModel`) for dependency injection, allowing the system to provide it automatically to the `HomeScreen`.\n- **State Management:** Uses Coroutines `StateFlow` to manage:\n  - `selectedLanguage`: Defaults to \"Telugu\".\n  - `selectedSource`: Defaults to \"all\".\n  - `isRecording`: Toggles between true/false.\n  - `uiState`: Tracks the screen phase using a sealed class (`HomeUiState`) with states like `Idle`, `Recording`, `Processing`, `NavigatingToAnswer`, and `Error`.\n- **Action Handlers:** Includes functions (`onMicPressed`, `onLanguageSelected`, `onSourceSelected`) that update these state flows. These act as placeholders for the real offline logic (like AudioRecord API access) that will be integrated in Phase 4.\n\n### Beginner Explanation\n\nThe ViewModel is like the \"waiter\" in a restaurant. The UI (the buttons and menus we just built) is the dining room. When a customer (user) taps a button, the UI tells the waiter \"I want to record.\" The waiter (ViewModel) writes this down on his notepad (`StateFlow`). Because the UI is always watching the waiter`s notepad, it instantly redraws the button in red as soon as the waiter writes \"Recording = true\". This separation means the button doesn`t have to know *how* to record audio; it only knows how to look when recording is happening.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task `P1.S3.SS1.MT5` is complete.\nThe HomeViewModel is ready.\nThe next micro-task is `P1.S3.SS1.MT6 — Build the Full HomeScreen Layout`.\n"
  }
}
```

Before we can display an answer on the screen, we need to know exactly what information makes up an \"answer.\" The \\`FatwaAnswer\\` data class tells Android that every answer *must* have an ID, a question, the actual text, and a web link to the source. \n\nBy creating a \\`PLACEHOLDER\\` object inside this class, we've filled out one of these forms with fake (but realistic-looking) Telugu text. This allows us to build and test the Answer Screen's design without needing to connect to the real database just yet.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task \\`P1.S4.SS1.MT1\\` is complete.\nThe data models for the Answer Screen are established.\nThe next micro-task is \\`P1.S4.SS1.MT2 — Create AnswerViewModel\\`.\nEOF\n"

## MODEL ✨

I have successfully completed micro-task **P1.S4.SS1.MT1** (Create Answer Data Classes).

### ✅ Accomplishments:
1.  **Created `AnswerModels.kt`:** I added the `FatwaAnswer` and `RelatedVideo` data classes.
2.  **Added Placeholder Data:** I implemented a companion object in `FatwaAnswer` with realistic Telugu text to serve as our `PLACEHOLDER`.
3.  **Compiled Successfully:** The new models compile without any issues.
4.  **Updated Documentation:** `ACTIVITY_LOG.md`, `SPRINT_SYSTEM.md`, and `02_ANDROID_APP_LAYER.md` have been updated.

---

### 🎓 Concept: ViewModel vs. Data Class
In the previous step, we built the **Data Class** (`FatwaAnswer`). As explained, this is just a blueprint.
Now, we need the **ViewModel** (`AnswerViewModel`). 

**Analogy:** If the data class is the *recipe* for a dish, the ViewModel is the *waiter* who holds the prepared dish and brings it to your table (the UI). The ViewModel makes sure that even if you rotate your phone (which destroys and recreates the UI), your dish (the data) isn't lost.

---

### 🏗️ Next Step: P1.S4.SS1.MT2 — Create AnswerViewModel

I will now create the `AnswerViewModel.kt` file which will hold our fake data and manage the state for the Answer Screen.**Tool Command**:

It will hold the `FatwaAnswer` and `RelatedVideo` data in `StateFlow` variables and provide placeholder functions for user interactions like reading aloud and clicking videos.",
    "strategic_intent": "Creating the AnswerViewModel to manage the state and data for the Answer Screen."

```**Tool Command**:
```json
{
  "id": "write_file__write_file_1780197597745_1",
  "name": "write_file",
  "args": {
    "file_path": "android-app/app/src/main/java/com/rushdululilm/app/viewmodel/AnswerViewModel.kt",
    "content": "package com.rushdululilm.app.viewmodel\n\n// File: AnswerViewModel.kt\n// Purpose: The \"brain\" of the Answer Screen. It holds the fatwa answer and related videos.\n// Layer: Layer 1 — Android App (ViewModel)\n// Depends on: AnswerModels.kt\n// Created: 2026-05-31 | Developer: Shaik Hidayatullah\n\nimport androidx.lifecycle.ViewModel\nimport com.rushdululilm.app.model.FatwaAnswer\nimport com.rushdululilm.app.model.RelatedVideo\nimport dagger.hilt.android.lifecycle.HiltViewModel\nimport kotlinx.coroutines.flow.MutableStateFlow\nimport kotlinx.coroutines.flow.StateFlow\nimport kotlinx.coroutines.flow.asStateFlow\nimport javax.inject.Inject\n\n/**\n * The ViewModel for the Answer Screen.\n * \n * @HiltViewModel tells Hilt to automatically construct this class for us when the screen needs it.\n * @Inject constructor() tells Hilt how to build it.\n */\n@HiltViewModel\nclass AnswerViewModel @Inject constructor() : ViewModel() {\n\n    // --- StateFlow Properties ---\n    // StateFlow acts as a \"live variable\". When its value updates, the UI automatically redraws.\n\n    // Holds the currently displayed fatwa answer. It starts as null, but we'll load the placeholder.\n    private val _currentAnswer = MutableStateFlow<FatwaAnswer?>(FatwaAnswer.PLACEHOLDER)\n    val currentAnswer: StateFlow<FatwaAnswer?> = _currentAnswer.asStateFlow()\n\n    // Holds a list of related video lectures to show at the bottom.\n    // For now, we populate it with 2 fake (placeholder) videos.\n    private val _relatedVideos = MutableStateFlow<List<RelatedVideo>>(\n        listOf(\n            RelatedVideo(\n                id = \"vid_01\",\n                title = \"Understanding Salah\",\n                scholarName = \"Fake Scholar 1\",\n                durationSeconds = 600, // 10 minutes\n                filePath = \"/fake/path/1.mp4\",\n                serverUrl = \"http://fake.server/1.mp4\"\n            ),\n            RelatedVideo(\n                id = \"vid_02\",\n                title = \"Traveling Rules\",\n                scholarName = \"Fake Scholar 2\",\n                durationSeconds = 1200, // 20 minutes\n                filePath = \"/fake/path/2.mp4\",\n                serverUrl = \"http://fake.server/2.mp4\"\n            )\n        )\n    )\n    val relatedVideos: StateFlow<List<RelatedVideo>> = _relatedVideos.asStateFlow()\n\n    // Tracks if the Text-To-Speech (TTS) engine is currently reading the answer out loud.\n    private val _isReadingAloud = MutableStateFlow(false)\n    val isReadingAloud: StateFlow<Boolean> = _isReadingAloud.asStateFlow()\n\n    // Tracks if we are currently waiting for the server/database to fetch the answer.\n    private val _isLoading = MutableStateFlow(false)\n    val isLoading: StateFlow<Boolean> = _isLoading.asStateFlow()\n\n    // --- User Actions ---\n\n    /**\n     * Called when the screen first opens to load a specific answer.\n     * @param answerId The unique ID of the answer we want to fetch.\n     */\n    fun loadAnswer(answerId: String) {\n        // Placeholder logic: For now, we just ensure the PLACEHOLDER is loaded.\n        // In the future, this will ask the database or server for the real answer.\n        _isLoading.value = true\n        _currentAnswer.value = FatwaAnswer.PLACEHOLDER\n        _isLoading.value = false\n        println(\"Loaded answer with ID: $answerId\") // Placeholder log\n    }\n\n    /**\n     * Called when the user taps the \"Read Aloud\" button.\n     */\n    fun onReadAloudPressed() {\n        // Placeholder logic: Toggle the visual state and print a message.\n        // Later, this will trigger the Android TTS or Coqui XTTS service.\n        _isReadingAloud.value = !_isReadingAloud.value\n        \n        if (_isReadingAloud.value) {\n            println(\"TTS: Started reading aloud...\")\n        } else {\n            println(\"TTS: Stopped reading aloud.\")\n        }\n    }\n\n    /**\n     * Called when the user taps one of the related video cards.\n     * @param video The video that was clicked.\n     */\n    fun onVideoClicked(video: RelatedVideo) {\n        // Placeholder logic: Print which video was clicked.\n        // Later, this will navigate to the Video Player screen.\n        println(\"Video clicked: ${video.title} by ${video.scholarName}\")\n    }\n}\n"
  }
}
```

MutableStateFlow(false)\n    val isReadingAloud: StateFlow<Boolean> = _isReadingAloud.asStateFlow()\n\n    // Tracks if we are currently waiting for the server/database to fetch the answer.\n    private val _isLoading = MutableStateFlow(false)\n    val isLoading: StateFlow<Boolean> = _isLoading.asStateFlow()\n\n    // --- User Actions ---\n\n    /**\n     * Called when the screen first opens to load a specific answer.\n     * @param answerId The unique ID of the answer we want to fetch.\n     */\n    fun loadAnswer(answerId: String) {\n        // Placeholder logic: For now, we just ensure the PLACEHOLDER is loaded.\n        // In the future, this will ask the database or server for the real answer.\n        _isLoading.value = true\n        _currentAnswer.value = FatwaAnswer.PLACEHOLDER\n        _isLoading.value = false\n        println(\"Loaded answer with ID: $answerId\") // Placeholder log\n    }\n\n    /**\n     * Called when the user taps the \"Read Aloud\" button.\n     */\n    fun onReadAloudPressed() {\n        // Placeholder logic: Toggle the visual state and print a message.\n        // Later, this will trigger the Android TTS or Coqui XTTS service.\n        _isReadingAloud.value = !_isReadingAloud.value\n        \n        if (_isReadingAloud.value) {\n            println(\"TTS: Started reading aloud...\")\n        } else {\n            println(\"TTS: Stopped reading aloud.\")\n        }\n    }\n\n    /**\n     * Called when the user taps one of the related video cards.\n     * @param video The video that was clicked.\n     */\n    fun onVideoClicked(video: RelatedVideo) {\n        // Placeholder logic: Print which video was clicked.\n        // Later, this will navigate to the Video Player screen.\n        println(\"Video clicked: ${video.title} by ${video.scholarName}\")\n    }\n}\n"
  }
}
```

### ✅ Accomplishments:

2.  **State Management:** I set up `StateFlow` properties to hold the `currentAnswer`, `relatedVideos`, `isReadingAloud`, and `isLoading`. These are currently populated with fake placeholder data for UI development.
3.  **Action Handlers:** I added placeholder functions for UI interactions like `loadAnswer`, `onReadAloudPressed`, and `onVideoClicked`.
4.  **Compiled Successfully:** The ViewModel compiles cleanly.
5.  **Updated Documentation:** `ACTIVITY_LOG.md`, `SPRINT_SYSTEM.md`, and `02_ANDROID_APP_LAYER.md` have been updated.

---

### 🎓 Concept: LazyColumn and LazyRow
The next task is to build the Answer Screen layout. We will need to display a long text answer and a list of related videos.

In Android, if you have a list of items (like videos) or a screen that might be too long to fit, you shouldn't draw everything at once. That wastes memory.
Instead, we use **`LazyColumn`** (for vertical scrolling) and **`LazyRow`** (for horizontal scrolling).

**Analogy:** A `LazyColumn` is like a film projector. It only projects (draws) the frames of the movie that are currently in front of the lens. As you scroll, it quickly erases the views that moved off-screen and draws the new ones coming on-screen. This makes scrolling very fast and efficient.

---

### 🏗️ Next Step: P1.S4.SS1.MT3 — Build AnswerScreen Layout

 // LocalContext is needed to start intents (like opening a web browser)\n    val context = LocalContext.current\n\n    Scaffold(\n        topBar = {\n            TopAppBar(\n                title = { Text(\"సమాధానం / Answer\") },\n                // Back button to return to the Home Screen\n                navigationIcon = {\n                    IconButton(onClick = { navController.popBackStack() }) {\n                        Icon(Icons.AutoMirrored.Filled.ArrowBack, contentDescription = \"Back\")\n                    }\n                },\n                colors = TopAppBarDefaults.topAppBarColors(\n                    containerColor = MaterialTheme.colorScheme.primary,\n                    titleContentColor = MaterialTheme.colorScheme.onPrimary,\n                    navigationIconContentColor = MaterialTheme.colorScheme.onPrimary\n                )\n            )\n        }\n    ) { innerPadding ->\n        // LazyColumn is used because answers might be longer than the screen.\n        // It only draws the content that is currently visible, saving memory.\n        LazyColumn(\n            modifier = Modifier\n                .fillMaxSize()\n                .padding(innerPadding)\n                .background(MaterialTheme.colorScheme.background),\n            contentPadding = PaddingValues(16.dp) // Add padding around all content inside the scroll\n        ) {\n            // We only show content if we actually have an answer to show\n            currentAnswer?.let { answer ->\n                \n                item {\n                    // Source Name Badge (e.g., IslamQA.info)\n                    Box(\n                        modifier = Modifier\n                            .clip(RoundedCornerShape(16.dp)) // Pill shape\n                            .background(QuranicBlue)\n                            .padding(horizontal = 12.dp, vertical = 6.dp)\n                    ) {\n                        Text(\n                            text = answer.sourceName,\n                            color = Color.White,\n                            style = MaterialTheme.typography.labelSmall // 16sp minimum\n                        )\n                    }\n\n                    Spacer(modifier = Modifier.height(16.dp))\n\n                    // The actual answer text. It should be large and readable.\n                    Text(\n                        text = answer.answerText,\n                        style = MaterialTheme.typography.bodyLarge, // 18sp minimum\n                        color = MaterialTheme.colorScheme.onBackground,\n                        lineHeight = 28.sp // Extra line height for readability\n                    )\n\n                    Spacer(modifier = Modifier.height(24.dp))\n\n                    // Clickable Source URL Row\n                    Row(verticalAlignment = Alignment.CenterVertically) {\n                        Text(\n                            text = \"మూలం / Source: \",\n                            style = MaterialTheme.typography.bodyMedium, // 16sp\n                            color = Color.Gray\n                        )\n                        \n                        // We build an \"Annotated String\" to make part of the text look like a clickable link\n                        val annotatedLinkString = buildAnnotatedString {\n                            withStyle(\n                                style = SpanStyle(\n                                    color = QuranicBlue,\n                                    textDecoration = TextDecoration.Underline,\n                                    fontSize = 16.sp\n                                )\n                            ) {\n                                append(\"View Original\")\n                            }\n                            // Attach the URL to this text segment\n                            addStringAnnotation(\n                                tag = \"URL\",\n                                annotation = answer.sourceUrl,\n                                start = 0,\n                                end = 13 // Length of \"View Original\"\n                            )\n                        }\n\n                        // ClickableText handles the tap event on the annotated string\n                        ClickableText(\n                            text = annotatedLinkString,\n                            onClick = { offset ->\n                                annotatedLinkString.getStringAnnotations(tag = \"URL\", start = offset, end = offset)\n                                    .firstOrNull()?.let { annotation ->\n                                        // Open the browser with the URL\n                                        val intent = Intent(Intent.ACTION_VIEW, Uri.parse(annotation.item))\n                                        context.startActivity(intent)\n                                    }\n                            }\n                        )\n                    }\n\n                    Spacer(modifier = Modifier.height(16.dp))\n                    HorizontalDivider() // A thin line separating sections\n                    Spacer(modifier = Modifier.height(16.dp))\n\n                    // Read Aloud Button - MUST be prominent and easily accessible\n                    Button(\n                        onClick = { answerViewModel.onReadAloudPressed() },\n                        modifier = Modifier\n                            .fillMaxWidth()\n                            .heightIn(min = 56.dp), // Large touch target\n                        // Change color if it is currently reading\n                        colors = ButtonDefaults.buttonColors(\n                            containerColor = if (isReadingAloud) Color.Gray else IslamicGreen\n                        )\n                    ) {\n                        Text(\n                            text = if (isReadingAloud) \"ఆపు / Stop Reading\" else \"🔊 చదివి వినిపించు / Read Aloud\",\n                            style = MaterialTheme.typography.bodyLarge // 18sp\n                        )\n                    }\n\n                    Spacer(modifier = Modifier.height(16.dp))\n                    HorizontalDivider()\n                    Spacer(modifier = Modifier.height(16.dp))\n\n                    // Related Videos Section Header\n                    Text(\n                        text = \"సంబంధిత వీడియోలు / Related Lectures\",\n                        style = MaterialTheme.typography.titleLarge, // 22sp bold\n                        color = MaterialTheme.colorScheme.onBackground\n                    )\n\n                    Spacer(modifier = Modifier.height(12.dp))\n                }\n\n                // Horizontal scrollable list for related videos\n                item {\n                    LazyRow(\n                        horizontalArrangement = Arrangement.spacedBy(16.dp), // Space between cards\n                        modifier = Modifier.fillMaxWidth()\n                    ) {\n                        items(relatedVideos) { video ->\n                            VideoCard(video = video, onPlayClick = { answerViewModel.onVideoClicked(video) })\n                        }\n                    }\n                }\n            }\n        }\n    }\n}\n\n/**\n * A reusable component to display a single video suggestion.\n */\n@Composable\nfun VideoCard(video: RelatedVideo, onPlayClick: () -> Unit) {\n    Card(\n        modifier = Modifier\n            .width(280.dp) // Fixed width so they look like horizontal cards\n            .shadow(4.dp, RoundedCornerShape(8.dp)),\n        colors = CardDefaults.cardColors(containerColor = MaterialTheme.colorScheme.surface)\n    ) {\n        Column(modifier = Modifier.padding(12.dp)) {\n            // Scholar Name\n            Text(\n                text = video.scholarName,\n                style = MaterialTheme.typography.labelSmall, // 16sp\n                color = IslamicGreen\n            )\n            Spacer(modifier = Modifier.height(4.dp))\n            // Video Title\n            Text(\n                text = video.title,\n                style = MaterialTheme.typography.bodyLarge.copy(fontWeight = FontWeight.Bold), // 18sp\n                color = MaterialTheme.colorScheme.onSurface,\n                maxLines = 2 // Prevent extremely long titles from breaking the layout\n            )\n            Spacer(modifier = Modifier.height(4.dp))\n            \n            // Format duration from seconds to MM:SS\n            val minutes = video.durationSeconds / 60\n            val seconds = video.durationSeconds % 60\n            Text(\n                text = String.format(\"%02d:%02d\", minutes, seconds),\n                style = MaterialTheme.typography.bodyMedium, // 16sp\n                color = Color.Gray\n            )\n            \n            Spacer(modifier = Modifier.height(8.dp))\n            \n            // Play Button\n            Button(\n                onClick = onPlayClick,\n                modifier = Modifier.fillMaxWidth(),\n                colors = ButtonDefaults.buttonColors(containerColor = QuranicBlue)\n            ) {\n                Text(\"▶ ప్లే చేయి / Play\", style = MaterialTheme.typography.bodyMedium)\n            }\n        }\n    }\n}\n"
  }

FontWeight.Bold), // 18sp\n                color = MaterialTheme.colorScheme.onSurface,\n                maxLines = 2 // Prevent extremely long titles from breaking the layout\n            )\n            Spacer(modifier = Modifier.height(4.dp))\n            \n            // Format duration from seconds to MM:SS\n            val minutes = video.durationSeconds / 60\n            val seconds = video.durationSeconds % 60\n            Text(\n                text = String.format(\"%02d:%02d\", minutes, seconds),\n                style = MaterialTheme.typography.bodyMedium, // 16sp\n                color = Color.Gray\n            )\n            \n            Spacer(modifier = Modifier.height(8.dp))\n            \n            // Play Button\n            Button(\n                onClick = onPlayClick,\n                modifier = Modifier.fillMaxWidth(),\n                colors = ButtonDefaults.buttonColors(containerColor = QuranicBlue)\n            ) {\n                Text(\"▶ ప్లే చేయి / Play\", style = MaterialTheme.typography.bodyMedium)\n            }\n        }\n    }\n}\n"

This screen is the destination after a user asks a question.\n\n- **Layout Structure:** It utilizes a \\`Scaffold\\` with a \\`TopAppBar\\` (containing a back button) and a \\`LazyColumn\\` for the main content body.\n- **Answer Display:** Shows a badge with the source name (e.g., \"IslamQA.info\") and the translated fatwa answer using the required 18sp minimum typography.\n- **Source Link:** A \\`ClickableText\\` element was added that opens the phone's web browser to the original fatwa URL when tapped. This satisfies a strict project requirement.\n- **Read Aloud:** A large, prominent button spanning the full width was added for the TTS feature. Its color dynamically changes based on the \\`isReadingAloud\\` state from the ViewModel.\n- **Related Videos:** A \\`LazyRow\\` was implemented at the bottom of the screen to display a horizontally scrolling list of \\`VideoCard\\` composables, showing the fake related video data.\n\n### Beginner Explanation\n\nThe Answer Screen is like a long scroll of paper. Because an Islamic answer can be very detailed, we can't just put it in a normal box on the screen; we have to make it scrollable. In Android, we use a \\`LazyColumn\\` for this. It's \"lazy\" because it only does the work of drawing the parts of the text that you are currently looking at.\n\nWe also added a \"Read Aloud\" button directly under the answer. This is crucial for users who cannot read well. Finally, at the very bottom, we added a \"carousel\" (a \\`LazyRow\\`) of related videos. This allows users to swipe left and right to see lectures related to their question without leaving the page.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task \\`P1.S4.SS1.MT3\\` is complete.\nSprint 1.4 is **COMPLETE**.\nThe next sprint is **Sprint 1.5 — Video Library Screen**.\nEOF\n"

text typed by the user.\n    private val _searchQuery = MutableStateFlow(\"\")\n    val searchQuery: StateFlow<String> = _searchQuery.asStateFlow()\n\n    // The list of videos currently shown on screen (filtered by search query).\n    // Initially, it shows all videos.\n    private val _filteredVideos = MutableStateFlow<List<RelatedVideo>>(fakeVideos)\n    val filteredVideos: StateFlow<List<RelatedVideo>> = _filteredVideos.asStateFlow()\n\n    // --- User Actions ---\n\n    /**\n     * Called whenever the user types something into the search bar.\n     * It updates the search text and filters the list of videos.\n     * \n     * @param query The text currently in the search bar.\n     */\n    fun onSearchQueryChanged(query: String) {\n        _searchQuery.value = query\n        \n        // Filter logic: if the query is empty, show all videos.\n        // Otherwise, check if the query is in the title or the scholar's name (ignoring uppercase/lowercase).\n        if (query.isBlank()) {\n            _filteredVideos.value = _allVideos.value\n        } else {\n            _filteredVideos.value = _allVideos.value.filter { video ->\n                video.title.contains(query, ignoreCase = true) || \n                video.scholarName.contains(query, ignoreCase = true)\n            }\n        }\n    }\n}\n",

\"/sdcard/videos/v5.mp4\",\n            serverUrl = \"http://fake.server/v5.mp4\"\n        )\n    )\n\n    // --- StateFlow Properties ---\n\n    // The complete list of videos (never changes in this fake setup).\n    private val _allVideos = MutableStateFlow<List<RelatedVideo>>(fakeVideos)\n    val allVideos: StateFlow<List<RelatedVideo>> = _allVideos.asStateFlow()\n\n    // The current search text typed by the user.\n    private val _searchQuery = MutableStateFlow(\"\")\n    val searchQuery: StateFlow<String> = _searchQuery.asStateFlow()\n\n    // The list of videos currently shown on screen (filtered by search query).\n    // Initially, it shows all videos.\n    private val _filteredVideos = MutableStateFlow<List<RelatedVideo>>(fakeVideos)\n    val filteredVideos: StateFlow<List<RelatedVideo>> = _filteredVideos.asStateFlow()\n\n    // --- User Actions ---\n\n    /**\n     * Called whenever the user types something into the search bar.\n     * It updates the search text and filters the list of videos.\n     * \n     * @param query The text currently in the search bar.\n     */\n    fun onSearchQueryChanged(query: String) {\n        _searchQuery.value = query\n        \n        // Filter logic: if the query is empty, show all videos.\n        // Otherwise, check if the query is in the title or the scholar's name (ignoring uppercase/lowercase).\n        if (query.isBlank()) {\n            _filteredVideos.value = _allVideos.value\n        } else {\n            _filteredVideos.value = _allVideos.value.filter { video ->\n                video.title.contains(query, ignoreCase = true) || \n                video.scholarName.contains(query, ignoreCase = true)\n            }\n        }\n    }\n}\n"

Because an Islamic answer can be very detailed, we can't just put it in a normal box on the screen; we have to make it scrollable. In Android, we use a `LazyColumn` for this. It's \"lazy\" because it only does the work of drawing the parts of the text that you are currently looking at.\n\nWe also added a \"Read Aloud\" button directly under the answer. This is crucial for users who cannot read well. Finally, at the very bottom, we added a \"carousel\" (a `LazyRow`) of related videos. This allows users to swipe left and right to see lectures related to their question without leaving the page.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task `P1.S4.SS1.MT3` is complete.\nSprint 1.4 is **COMPLETE**.\nThe next sprint is **Sprint 1.5 — Video Library Screen**.\n"

The \\`VideoLibraryViewModel\\` acts like a helpful librarian.\n\nWhen the screen first opens, the librarian holds the complete catalog of videos (\\`allVideos\\`) and places them all on the display shelf (\\`filteredVideos\\`). When the user starts typing a name into the search bar, the UI tells the librarian what was typed (\\`searchQuery\\`). The librarian quickly checks every video's title and scholar name, picks out the ones that match, and updates the display shelf so the user only sees the relevant videos. We've stocked the library with 5 fake videos for now so we can test the search feature.\n\n### Result\n\nMicro-task \\`P1.S5.SS1.MT1\\` is complete.\nThe VideoLibraryViewModel is ready.\nThe next micro-task is \\`P1.S5.SS1.MT2 — Build VideoLibraryScreen Layout\\`.",

---

### 🎓 Concept: Filtering Lists in Kotlin
In the ViewModel, we used a very common Kotlin function called `filter`. 

**Analogy**: Imagine you have a basket of mixed fruit (`allVideos`). When the user types "apple" into the search bar, the `filter` function acts like a person who picks up every piece of fruit one by one, checks if it's an apple, and puts it into a new, smaller basket (`filteredVideos`). The UI then looks at this smaller basket and displays only the apples. This happens instantly every time a letter is typed!

Open terminal\n  2. Check if KVM is already enabled: ls /dev/kvm\n     - If you see /dev/kvm → KVM is ready, skip to step 5\n     - If \"No such file\" → continue to step 3\n  3. Install KVM: sudo apt install qemu-kvm libvirt-daemon-system libvirt-clients\n  4. Add your user to the kvm group: sudo usermod -aG kvm $USER\n  5. Reboot Parrot OS: sudo reboot\n  6. After reboot, verify: ls -la /dev/kvm\n     Should show: crw-rw---- 1 root kvm ...\n\nDONE CONDITION:\n  Terminal shows /dev/kvm exists after reboot.\n  Command 'kvm-ok' (install with: sudo apt install cpu-checker) shows:\n  \"INFO: /dev/kvm exists. KVM acceleration can be used.\"\n\nNEXT MICRO-TASK: P1.S1.SS1.MT3\n```\n\n##### Micro-task P1.S1.SS1.MT3 — Create Android Virtual Device (Emulator)\n```\nTASK:   Create an Android emulator (virtual phone) inside Android Studio\n\nWHY:    We need a virtual phone to test the app without a real phone.\n        We'll use a Pixel 5 emulator running Android 12 for testing.\n\nSTEP BY STEP:\n  1. Open Android Studio\n  2. Click: Tools → Device Manager\n  3. Click: \"Create Device\" button\n  4. Choose phone: Select \"Pixel 5\" → Next\n  5. Choose Android version: Select \"API 31 (Android 12)\" → Download if needed → Next\n  6. AVD Name: leave as default \"Pixel_5_API_31\"\n  7. Click Finish\n  8. Click the ▶ Play button next to the new device to start the emulator\n  9. Wait for the Android home screen to appear (may take 2-3 minutes first time)\n\nDONE CONDITION:\n  The Android emulator window opens and shows the Android home screen.\n  You can swipe and interact with it like a real phone.\n\nNEXT MICRO-TASK: P1.S1.SS2.MT1\n```\n\n---\n\n#### Sub-sprint 1.1.2 — Create the Android Studio Project\n\n##### Micro-task P1.S1.SS2.MT1 — Create New Android Project\n```\nTASK:   Create the Rushd-ul-Ilm Android project in Android Studio\n\nCRITICAL: Package name MUST be exactly: com.rushdululilm.app — never change this\n\nSTEP BY STEP:\n  1. Jetpack Compose BOM (latest) — manages all Compose library versions\n  - Compose UI + Material3 — UI components and Google's design system\n  - Compose ViewModel + Runtime — MVVM ViewModel integration with Compose\n  - Hilt (DI) — dependency injection — hilt-android + hilt-compiler\n  - Room (SQLite) — local database — room-runtime + room-ktx + room-compiler + room-testing\n  - Retrofit + OkHttp — HTTP client for calling FastAPI server\n  - Gson converter — converts JSON responses to Kotlin data classes\n  - ExoPlayer / Media3 — video player\n  - Navigation Compose — handles navigation between screens\n  - Coroutines — for async operations (network calls, database)\n  - WorkManager — background sync of offline databases\n  - ONNX Runtime Android 1.17.3 — runs Opus-MT offline translation on phone\n\nDONE CONDITION:\n  Gradle syncs without errors after saving the file.\n  \"BUILD SUCCESSFUL\" shown in the Build tab.\n  No red squiggly lines in build.gradle.kts.\n\nNEXT MICRO-TASK: P1.S1.SS2.MT3\n```\n\n##### Micro-task P1.S1.SS2.MT3 — Configure Hilt in Application Class\n```\nTASK:   Set up Hilt Dependency Injection — create the Application class\n\nWHY HILT EXISTS (explain this before any code):\n  In Android, many classes need the same objects — for example, every screen\n  might need the database, the network client, and the ViewModel.\n  Without Hilt, you'd have to manually create and pass these objects everywhere.\n  Hilt is like a smart factory — you tell it what objects exist once,\n  and it automatically provides them wherever they're needed.\n  This is called \"Dependency Injection\" (DI).\n\nFILES TO CREATE:\n  1. android-app/app/src/main/java/com/rushdululilm/app/RushdulIlmApplication.kt\n     - Add @HiltAndroidApp annotation on the Application class"

  Card(\n        modifier = Modifier\n            .width(280.dp) // Fixed width so they look like horizontal cards\n            .shadow(4.dp, RoundedCornerShape(8.dp)),\n        colors = CardDefaults.cardColors(containerColor = MaterialTheme.colorScheme.surface)\n    ) {\n        Column(modifier = Modifier.padding(12.dp)) {\n            // Scholar Name\n            Text(\n                text = video.scholarName,\n                style = MaterialTheme.typography.labelSmall, // 16sp\n                color = IslamicGreen\n            )\n            Spacer(modifier = Modifier.height(4.dp))\n            // Video Title\n            Text(\n                text = video.title,\n                style = MaterialTheme.typography.bodyLarge.copy(fontWeight = FontWeight.Bold), // 18sp\n                color = MaterialTheme.colorScheme.onSurface,\n                maxLines = 2 // Prevent extremely long titles from breaking the layout\n            )\n            Spacer(modifier = Modifier.height(4.dp))\n            \n            // Format duration from seconds to MM:SS\n            val minutes = video.durationSeconds / 60\n            val seconds = video.durationSeconds % 60\n            Text(\n                text = String.format(\"%02d:%02d\", minutes, seconds),\n                style = MaterialTheme.typography.bodyMedium, // 16sp\n                color = Color.Gray\n            )\n            "
  }
}

--- Content from referenced files ---
Content from @knowledge-graph/GRAPHITI_SETUP.md:
# GRAPHITI_SETUP.md
# Rushd-ul-Ilm (رشد العلم) — Graphiti Knowledge Graph Setup Guide
# Developer: Shaik Hidayatullah, Kurnool, Andhra Pradesh, India
# Last Updated: 2026-05-28
#
# ════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
# WHAT IS GRAPHITI?
# Graphiti (github.com/getzep/graphiti) is an open-source Python library
# that builds a "knowledge graph" — think of it like a living map of your
# entire project. It remembers:
#   - What decisions were made (and WHEN)
#   - What files exist and how they connect to each other
#   - What each AI agent session completed
#   - What Islamic sources have been indexed
#   - What the next task is
#
# WHY DO WE NEED IT?
# When you switch from one AI agent to another (Claude → Gemini → Codex),
# the new agent normally has NO memory of your project. With Graphiti,
# any new agent can query the knowledge graph and instantly know
# the full project state — without you having to explain everything again.
#
# COST: 100% FREE — runs locally on your Ubuntu machine using Ollama
# ════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

---

## 🧠 What is a Knowledge Graph? (Plain English)

A knowledge graph is like a mind map, but for your entire project.

**Regular database example:** "Video file X exists"
**Knowledge graph example:** "Video file X was transcribed on 2026-05-15 by faster-whisper,
the transcript has 1,247 tokens, it belongs to scholar Mufti Menk, and it was added to
Qdrant collection 'islamic_videos'. The decision to use faster-whisper instead of Google
Speech API was made on 2026-05-10 because of privacy requirements."

Graphiti stores not just WHAT things are, but HOW they relate and WHEN things happened.
This is called a "temporal knowledge graph" — temporal means time-aware.

---

## 🏗️ Architecture Overview

```
Your Ubuntu Machine (Parrot OS / Ubuntu)
│
├── Neo4j (Graph Database) — stores the actual knowledge graph
│   Port: 7474 (web browser UI), Port: 7687 (app connection)
│   Docker container: neo4j:5-community (FREE version)
│
├── Graphiti (Python library) — reads/writes to Neo4j
│   Installed via: pip install graphiti-core
│   Your Python scripts import and use Graphiti
│
├── Ollama (Local LLM runtime)
│   Port: 11434 (already running for your main app)
│   Model: qwen3:4b (same model used for Islamic Q&A)
│   Graphiti uses Ollama to extract facts from text (no API key needed)
│
└── graphiti_helper.py (Your custom script — created in this guide)
    Functions: save_session(), search_project(), add_decision(), etc.
```

---

## ⚙️ STEP-BY-STEP INSTALLATION

### Step 1: Add Neo4j to Your Docker Compose
**Note:** This step is already implemented and performed .so no need to do it again
Open your `backend/docker-compose.yml` file and add this service block:

```yaml
services:
  neo4j:
    # Neo4j is the graph database that stores all Graphiti knowledge
    # neo4j:5-community is the FREE community edition — no license needed
    image: neo4j:5-community

    container_name: rushd-neo4j
    # ^ Give it a memorable name so we can reference it easily

    restart: unless-stopped
    # ^ Auto-restart if it crashes, but stop when you manually run 'docker compose down'

    ports:
      - "7474:7474"
      # ^ Port 7474 is the Neo4j web browser UI — open http://localhost:7474 to see your graph
      - "7687:7687"
      # ^ Port 7687 is the Bolt protocol port — Graphiti Python library connects here

    environment:
      - NEO4J_AUTH=neo4j/rushdululilm2026
      # ^ Sets username=neo4j, password=rushdululilm2026
      # ^ CHANGE THIS PASSWORD if you ever expose this server to the internet
      - NEO4J_PLUGINS=["apoc"]
      # ^ APOC is a plugin that adds extra features to Neo4j (needed by Graphiti)
      - NEO4J_dbms_security_procedures_unrestricted=apoc.*
      # ^ Allows APOC procedures to run — required for Graphiti to work correctly

    volumes:
      - neo4j_data:/data
      # ^ Stores the graph database files permanently (survives container restarts)
      - neo4j_logs:/logs
      # ^ Stores Neo4j logs for debugging

volumes:
  # Add these to your existing volumes section (or create the section if it doesn't exist)
  neo4j_data:
    # ^ Docker will create and manage this storage volume automatically
  neo4j_logs:
    # ^ Docker will create and manage this storage volume automatically
```

### Step 2: Start Neo4j
**Note:** This step is already implemented and performed .so no need to do it again
```bash
# Navigate to your backend folder
cd /home/hidayat/Documents/Islamic\ Knowledge\ Q\&A\ App/backend/

# Start ALL services including the new Neo4j container
# The -d flag means "detached" — runs in background, not blocking your terminal
docker compose up -d neo4j

# Check that Neo4j started successfully
docker compose logs neo4j
# ^ You should see: "Started." in the logs — this means Neo4j is running

# Wait about 30 seconds for Neo4j to fully start, then open the web UI
# Open your browser and go to: http://localhost:7474
# Username: neo4j
# Password: rushdululilm2026
# If you see a graph database UI — Neo4j is working! ✅
```

### Step 3: Install Graphiti Python Library
**Note:** This step is already implemented and performed .so no need to do it again
```bash
# Make sure you are NOT inside a Docker container — run this on your Ubuntu host machine
# (Open a normal terminal in Parrot OS)

# Create a Python virtual environment specifically for Graphiti
# A virtual environment is like a clean isolated box for Python packages
# so they don't interfere with other Python projects on your machine
python3 -m venv /home/hidayat/Documents/Islamic\ Knowledge\ Q\&A\ App/knowledge-graph/venv
# ^ python3 -m venv → use Python's built-in venv (virtual environment) tool
# ^ The path at the end is where the virtual environment folder will be created

# Activate the virtual environment
# IMPORTANT: You must activate it every time you open a new terminal
source /home/hidayat/Documents/Islamic\ Knowledge\ Q\&A\ App/knowledge-graph/venv/bin/activate
# ^ 'source' runs the activate script, which sets up your terminal to use this Python
# ^ After running this, your terminal prompt will show (venv) at the start

# Install Graphiti and its required packages
pip install graphiti-core
# ^ graphiti-core is the main Graphiti library from getzep/graphiti on GitHub

pip install neo4j
# ^ Python driver for connecting to Neo4j database

pip install httpx
# ^ HTTP library needed by Graphiti for some operations

# Verify installation
pip show graphiti-core
# ^ Should show: Name: graphiti-core, Version: [version number]
```

### Step 4: Configure Graphiti to Use Ollama (FREE — No API Key)
**Note:** This step is already implemented and performed .so no need to do it again
Here is the created file that uses Google Gemini's free tier API key, and ollama's nomic-embed-text model: `knowledge-graph/graphiti_config.py`



### Step 5: Install Ollama Embedding Model
**Note:** This step is already implemented and performed .so no need to do it again
```bash
# Pull the nomic-embed-text embedding model (it's free and small — ~270MB)
# Make sure Ollama is running first
ollama pull nomic-embed-text
# ^ 'ollama pull' downloads a model — like 'pip install' but for AI models

# Verify it downloaded
ollama list
# ^ Should show: nomic-embed-text in the list alongside qwen3:4b
```

### Step 6: Create the Graphiti Helper Script

Here is the created file that uses Google Gemini's free tier API key, and ollama's nomic-embed-text model: `knowledge-graph/graphiti_helper.py`

## 🧪 TESTING GRAPHITI
**Note:** This step is already implemented and performed .so no need to do it again
After setup, test everything with these commands:

```bash
# Navigate to the knowledge-graph folder
cd /home/hidayat/Documents/Islamic\ Knowledge\ Q\&A\ App/knowledge-graph/

# Activate the virtual environment
source venv/bin/activate
# ^ Your prompt should show (venv) now

# First: initialize Graphiti's required indexes in Neo4j
export $GEMINI_API_KEY=AQ.Ab8RN6KImltPQFfFLL2kg2arCOthkDWzPGP2nd3Nm-cZpwi96Q
export $OPENAI_API_KEY=ollama

python3 -c "
import asyncio
from graphiti_config import create_graphiti_client
async def init():
    client = create_graphiti_client()
    await client.build_indices_and_constraints()
    # ^ This creates the required database structure in Neo4j (only needed once)
    print('✅ Graphiti indexes created successfully')
    await client.close()
asyncio.run(init())
"

# Test saving a session
python graphiti_helper.py save-session "SETUP SESSION: Installed Graphiti and Neo4j. Configured Ollama. Ready to begin Phase 1 Android UI."

# Test searching (should return what we just saved)
python graphiti_helper.py search "current project status"

# Test adding a decision
python graphiti_helper.py add-decision "Use Neo4j Community Edition" "Free, open-source, fully self-hosted, no data sent to cloud, exactly fits our privacy requirements"
```

## 🔗 HOW AI AGENTS USE GRAPHITI

At the **START** of every coding session, the agent runs:
```bash
cd /home/hidayat/Documents/Islamic\ Knowledge\ Q\&A\ App/knowledge-graph/
source venv/bin/activate
export $GEMINI_API_KEY=AQ.Ab8RN6KImltPQFfFLL2kg2arCOthkDWzPGP2nd3Nm-cZpwi96Q
export $OPENAI_API_KEY=ollama
python graphiti_helper.py search "current phase next task"
python graphiti_helper.py search "files created last session"
```

At the **END** of every coding session, the agent runs:
```bash
python graphiti_helper.py save-session "Phase [N]: Created [files]. [Description of what was built]. Next task: [specific next step]."
```

   ```

## 🌐 Using Google's Free APIs Instead of Ollama (This one is already implemented in the graphiti_config.py script to use gemini instead of ollama) 
**Note:** This step is already implemented and performed .so no need to do it again
If Ollama is too slow on your machine for Graphiti's fact extraction, you can use
Google Gemini's FREE API tier instead:

```bash
# Get your FREE Google Gemini API key from:
# https://aistudio.google.com/apikey
# (No credit card needed for the free tier)

# The free tier as of 2026 includes:
# - Gemini 1.5 Flash: 15 requests per minute, 1 million tokens per minute (FREE)
# - More than enough for Graphiti's background fact extraction

pip install google-generativeai
# ^ Install Google's Python library
```

```python
# Modified graphiti_config.py for Google Gemini (free tier)

from graphiti_core.llm_client.gemini_client import GeminiClient
# ^ Graphiti has a built-in Gemini client — no workarounds needed

GOOGLE_API_KEY = "YOUR_FREE_API_KEY_FROM_AISTUDIO"
# ^ Replace with your key from https://aistudio.google.com/apikey

llm_client = GeminiClient(
    config=LLMConfig(
        model="gemini-1.5-flash",  # FREE model
        api_key=GOOGLE_API_KEY,
    )
)
# ^ Use this llm_client instead of the Ollama-based one in graphiti_config.py
# ^ Everything else in graphiti_config.py stays the same
# ^ Note: Islamic Q&A answers still use local Ollama — only Graphiti uses Google
# ^ Your Islamic Q&A data never goes to Google — only project metadata does
```

---

## ❓ TROUBLESHOOTING

**Problem:** `Connection refused` when running graphiti_helper.py
**Cause:** Neo4j container is not running
**Fix:**
```bash
docker compose up -d neo4j
docker compose logs neo4j  # Wait for "Started." message
```

**Problem:** `Model not found: nomic-embed-text`
**Cause:** Embedding model not downloaded
**Fix:**
```bash
ollama pull nomic-embed-text
```

**Problem:** Graphiti seems slow at extracting facts
**Cause:** Qwen3:4b is doing LLM inference — takes a few seconds per episode
**Fix:** This is normal. Graphiti runs in the background — it won't block your coding.
         Alternatively, switch to Google Gemini free tier (faster).

# MEM0_SETUP.md
# Rushd-ul-Ilm (رشد العلم) — Mem0 Self-Hosted Setup Guide
# Developer: Shaik Hidayatullah, Kurnool, Andhra Pradesh, India
# Last Updated: 2026-05-28
#
# ════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
# WHAT IS MEM0?
# Mem0 (pronounced "mem-zero") is an open-source memory layer for AI agents.
# Think of it like a "memory" for your AI coding assistants.
#
# WITHOUT Mem0:
#   - Claude helps you build HomeScreen.kt → session ends
#   - New Claude session starts → it remembers NOTHING
#   - You have to explain the entire project again from scratch
#
# WITH Mem0:
#   - Claude builds HomeScreen.kt → automatically saves key facts to Mem0
#   - New Claude session starts → reads Mem0 → knows your name, the project,
#     last task completed, your preferences, and where to continue
#   - You say "continue the project" and it knows exactly what to do
#
# DIFFERENCE FROM GRAPHITI:
#   Mem0: Personal facts, preferences, session summaries (short, fast retrieval)
#   Graphiti: Full project knowledge graph with timestamps and relationships (rich, deep)
#   USE BOTH: They complement each other
#
# COST: 100% FREE — self-hosted using Docker + Ollama
# ════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

---

## 🧠 What Mem0 Remembers (Examples)

```
"Developer prefers Kotlin explanations with Linux analogies"
"Developer is a beginner in Android Studio — always explain the IDE steps"
"Phase 1 is in progress. Last completed: NavGraph.kt"
"HomeScreen.kt is at android-app/app/src/main/java/com/rushdululilm/app/ui/screens/"
"Claude agent: explain Jetpack Compose before showing any Compose code"
"The developer's Ubuntu machine IP is 192.168.1.100"
"GTX 1650ti has 4GB VRAM — never suggest models above 3.5GB"
"Developer's primary OS is Parrot OS Linux — all bash commands should be for Linux"
```

---

## 🏗️ Architecture Overview

```
Your Ubuntu Machine
│
├── Qdrant (already running for Islamic Q&A on port 6333)
│   ^ Mem0 uses Qdrant to store memory embeddings
│   ^ No extra setup needed — same Qdrant instance
│
├── Ollama (already running on port 11434)
│   ^ Mem0 uses Ollama to extract key facts from conversations
│   ^ Uses qwen3:4b — same model as your Islamic Q&A
│   ^ NO API KEY NEEDED
│
├── Mem0 Server (new Docker service on port 8100)
│   ^ REST API that AI agents call to save/retrieve memories
│   ^ Runs as a Docker container
│
└── mem0_helper.py (your custom script)
    Functions: save_memory(), search_memory(), get_all_memories()
```

---

## ⚙️ STEP-BY-STEP INSTALLATION

### Option A: Self-Hosted Mem0 via Docker (Recommended)
**Note:** This option is not used and implemented and it will not be used in the future and instead, the python version will be used.
#### Step 1: Add Mem0 to Docker Compose

Add this to your `backend/docker-compose.yml`:

```yaml
# ─── Add this to your existing docker-compose.yml ───────────────────────────

  mem0:
    # Mem0 open-source server — self-hosted memory for AI agents
    image: mem0ai/mem0-server:latest
    # ^ Official Mem0 Docker image from Docker Hub

    container_name: rushd-mem0
    restart: unless-stopped

    ports:
      - "8100:8100"
      # ^ Port 8100 is where the Mem0 REST API will be available
      # ^ AI agents call http://localhost:8100 to save/retrieve memories

    environment:
      - MEM0_TELEMETRY=false
      # ^ Disable telemetry — no data sent to Mem0's servers

      - VECTOR_STORE_PROVIDER=qdrant
      # ^ Use our existing Qdrant container for storing memory vectors
      # ^ This means memories are searchable by meaning (semantic search)

      - QDRANT_HOST=qdrant
      # ^ 'qdrant' is the Docker service name of our Qdrant container
      # ^ Docker Compose automatically handles inter-container networking by service name

      - QDRANT_PORT=6333
      # ^ The port Qdrant listens on inside the Docker network

      - LLM_PROVIDER=ollama
      # ^ Use Ollama (local, free) instead of OpenAI to extract facts from text

      - OLLAMA_HOST=http://host.docker.internal:11434
      # ^ 'host.docker.internal' is a special Docker hostname that points to your Ubuntu machine
      # ^ This lets the Mem0 container reach Ollama which is running on your Ubuntu host

      - OLLAMA_MODEL=qwen3:4b
      # ^ Use Qwen3:4b for fact extraction — same model used for Islamic Q&A

      - EMBEDDER_PROVIDER=ollama
      # ^ Use Ollama for creating memory embeddings too

      - EMBEDDER_MODEL=nomic-embed-text
      # ^ Use the same embedding model we set up for Graphiti

    depends_on:
      - qdrant
      # ^ Wait for Qdrant to start before starting Mem0
      # ^ Mem0 needs Qdrant to store its memory vectors

    volumes:
      - mem0_data:/app/data
      # ^ Persistent storage for Mem0's configuration and SQLite metadata

volumes:
  # Add this to your existing volumes section
  mem0_data:
    # ^ Docker-managed storage for Mem0 data
```

#### Step 2: Start Mem0

```bash
# Start Mem0 (and Qdrant if not already running)
docker compose up -d qdrant mem0
# ^ Start both Qdrant and Mem0 in the background

# Check logs to confirm Mem0 started
docker compose logs mem0
# ^ Should see: "Mem0 server started on port 8100"

# Test that Mem0 API is working
curl http://localhost:8100/health
# ^ Should return: {"status": "ok"}
# ^ If you see this — Mem0 is running! ✅
```

### Option B: Mem0 Python Library (Simpler — No Extra Docker Container and currently this option is implemented)
**Note:** This option is already implemented. so no need to do it again
If you want to avoid another Docker container, use the Python library directly:

```bash
# Install Mem0 Python library in your knowledge-graph virtual environment
source /home/hidayat/Documents/Islamic\ Knowledge\ Q\&A\ App/knowledge-graph/venv/bin/activate
pip install mem0ai
# ^ 'mem0ai' is the official Python package name for Mem0
```

---

### Step 3: Create the Mem0 Helper Script
**Note:** This step is already implemented.so no need to do it again
Here is the created file that uses Google Gemini's free tier API key, qdrant, and ollama's nomic-embed-text model: `knowledge-graph/mem0_helper.py`

## 🧪 TESTING MEM0
**Note:** This step is already implemented and performed .so no need to do it again
```bash
# Navigate to knowledge-graph folder
cd /home/hidayat/Documents/Islamic\ Knowledge\ Q\&A\ App/knowledge-graph/

# Activate virtual environment
source venv/bin/activate

# Install mem0ai if not done yet
pip install mem0ai

# Test saving a session memory
python mem0_helper.py save "PROJECT SETUP COMPLETE. All rules files created. Phase 1 Android UI is next. Developer is beginner in Android Studio. Machine: Parrot OS Linux, GTX 1650ti 4GB VRAM."

# Test saving developer preferences
python mem0_helper.py save-preference "Always explain what a new Kotlin keyword does before using it in code"
python mem0_helper.py save-preference "Developer knows Python — use Python analogies to explain Kotlin concepts"
python mem0_helper.py save-preference "Developer is on Parrot OS Linux — all commands should use Linux syntax"

# Test searching
python mem0_helper.py search "current project status"
python mem0_helper.py search "developer preferences"

# List all memories
python mem0_helper.py list
```

---

## 🔄 HOW AI AGENTS USE MEM0

**At the START of every session:**
```bash
# Run these two commands in the knowledge-graph folder
python mem0_helper.py search "current phase next task"
python mem0_helper.py search "developer preferences"
```

**At the END of every session:**
```bash
python mem0_helper.py save "Phase [N]: [What was completed]. Files created: [list]. Next: [exact next task]."
```

---

## 🔑 USING FREE GOOGLE GEMINI API FOR MEM0 (This Faster Alternative is already implemented in the mem0_helper.py script to replace ollamas)

If Ollama-based extraction is slow, use Google Gemini's free tier:

```python
# In mem0_helper.py, replace the llm config section with:
"llm": {
    "provider": "gemini",
    "config": {
        "model": "gemini-2.5-flash",       # FREE model
        "api_key": "YOUR_FREE_KEY_FROM_AISTUDIO",
        # ^ Get free key at: https://aistudio.google.com/apikey
        # ^ Free tier: 15 requests/minute — more than enough for Mem0
    },
},
# Note: Islamic Q&A answers still use local Ollama
# Only Mem0's fact extraction uses Gemini — your user data stays private
```

---

## ❓ TROUBLESHOOTING

**Problem:** `Connection refused at localhost:8100`
**Cause:** Mem0 Docker container not running
**Fix:** `docker compose up -d mem0`

**Problem:** `Model qwen3:4b not found` in Mem0 logs
**Cause:** Ollama doesn't have the model downloaded
**Fix:** `ollama pull qwen3:4b`

**Problem:** Memories not found when searching
**Cause:** Different user_id used when saving vs searching
**Fix:** Always use the same DEVELOPER_ID constant — it's defined at the top of mem0_helper.py

**Problem:** Mem0 is slow at extracting memories
**Cause:** Qwen3:4b inference takes a few seconds per memory
**Fix:** This is normal. Mem0 processes memories in the background.
         Switch to Google Gemini free tier for faster extraction.

trick for embeddings — use OpenAI format, point to Ollama\n\nfrom graphiti_core.llm_client.config import LLMConfig\n# ^ LLMConfig lets us set which model and which server to use\n# Modified graphiti_config.py for Google Gemini (free tier)\nfrom graphiti_core.llm_client.gemini_client import GeminiClient\n# ^ Graphiti has a built-in Gemini client — no workarounds needed\n\nGOOGLE_API_KEY = os.environ.get(\"GEMINI_API_KEY\", \"\")\n# ^ Replace with your key from https://aistudio.google.com/apikey\n\n\n# ─── Ollama Configuration ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────\nOLLAMA_BASE_URL = \"http://localhost:11434/v1\"\n# ^ This is Ollama's OpenAI-compatible endpoint\n# ^ Port 11434 is where Ollama runs on your Ubuntu machine\n# ^ '/v1' is the API version path that Ollama exposes\n\nOLLAMA_API_KEY = \"ollama\"\n# ^ Ollama doesn't need a real API key, but the library requires SOMETHING here\n# ^ The word \"ollama\" is just a placeholder — it doesn't matter what this says\n\nOLLAMA_MODEL = \"phi4-mini:3.8b\"\n# ^ Use Qwen3:4b — the same model used for Islamic Q&A\n# ^ This model will extract facts from your project notes and session logs\n# ^ It fits in 4GB VRAM (2.5GB actual usage)\n\nEMBEDDING_MODEL = \"nomic-embed-text\"\n# ^ nomic-embed-text is a free embedding model available via Ollama\n# ^ Much smaller than the multilingual model — good for project notes\n# ^ Install with: ollama pull nomic-embed-text\n\nNEO4J_URI = \"bolt://localhost:7687\"\n# ^ bolt:// is Neo4j's own protocol (faster than HTTP for database queries)\n# ^ Port 7687 is the Neo4j Bolt port we exposed in docker-compose.yml\n\nNEO4J_USER = \"neo4j\"\n# ^ Default Neo4j username — must match what we set in docker-compose.yml\n\nNEO4J_PASSWORD = \"rushdululilm2026\"\n# ^ Must match the password we set in NEO4J_AUTH in docker-compose.yml\n\n\ndef create_graphiti_client():\n    \"\"\"\n    Creates and returns a configured Graphiti client that uses:\n    - Neo4j on localhost:7687 (graph storage)\n    - Ollama on localhost:11434 (LLM for fact extraction — NO API KEY NEEDED)\n    - nomic-embed-text via Ollama (embeddings — NO API KEY NEEDED)\n\n    Returns:\n        Graphiti: A ready-to-use Graphiti client object\n    \"\"\"\n\n    # Configure the LLM (the AI model that will read text and extract facts)\n    llm_config = LLMConfig(\n        base_url=OLLAMA_BASE_URL,\n        # ^ Tell Graphiti to use Ollama's API instead of OpenAI's\n        model=OLLAMA_MODEL,\n        # ^ Use Qwen3:4b model for fact extraction\n        api_key=OLLAMA_API_KEY,\n        # ^ Fake API key — Ollama doesn't check this\n    )\n\n\n    # Create the LLM client using OpenAI format but pointing to Ollama\n    llm_client = OpenAIGenericClient(config=llm_config)\n    #llm_client = GeminiClient(\n    #config=LLMConfig(\n    #    model=\"gemini-2.5-flash\",  # FREE model\n    #    api_key=GOOGLE_API_KEY,))\n    # ^ OpenAIClient is just a wrapper that knows how to make API calls\n    # ^ We configured it to call Ollama instead of OpenAI — it doesn't know the difference\n\n    # Configure the embedding model (converts text to numbers for similarity search)\n    embedder = OpenAIEmbedder(\n        config=OpenAIEmbedderConfig(\n            base_url=OLLAMA_BASE_URL,\n            # ^ Same Ollama server, different model\n            embedding_model=EMBEDDING_MODEL,\n            # ^ Use nomic-embed-text for creating text embeddings\n            api_key=OLLAMA_API_KEY,\n            # ^ Same fake API key\n        )\n    )\n\n    # Create and return the Graphiti client\n    graphiti_client = Graphiti(\n        uri=NEO4J_URI,\n        # ^ Where to find the Neo4j graph database\n        user=NEO4J_USER,\n        # ^ Neo4j login username\n        password=NEO4J_PASSWORD,\n        # ^ Neo4j login password\n        llm_client=llm_client,\n        # ^ The AI model that will read and extract facts from our notes\n        embedder=embedder,\n        # ^ The model that converts text to numbers for similarity matching\n    )\n\n    return graphiti_client\n    # ^ Return the ready-to-use client\n"
  }
}
```
